      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Economist_May_16_1846.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1638" data-file-width="1064" height="339" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/The_Economist_May_16_1846.png/220px-The_Economist_May_16_1846.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/The_Economist_May_16_1846.png/330px-The_Economist_May_16_1846.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/The_Economist_May_16_1846.png/440px-The_Economist_May_16_1846.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Economist_May_16_1846.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Front page of
         <i>
          The Economist
         </i>
         on 16 May 1846
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       was founded by the British businessman and banker
       <a href="/wiki/James_Wilson_(businessman)" title="James Wilson (businessman)">
        James Wilson
       </a>
       in 1843, to advance the repeal of the
       <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">
        Corn Laws
       </a>
       , a system of import tariffs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Prospectus_(finance)" title="Prospectus (finance)">
        prospectus
       </a>
       for the "newspaper" from 5 August 1843 enumerated thirteen areas of coverage that its editors wanted the publication to focus on:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        Original
        <a href="/wiki/Editorial" title="Editorial">
         leading articles
        </a>
        , in which free-trade principles will be most rigidly applied to all the important questions of the day.
       </li>
       <li>
        Articles relating to some practical, commercial, agricultural, or foreign topic of passing interest, such as foreign treaties.
       </li>
       <li>
        An article on the elementary principles of
        <a href="/wiki/Political_economy" title="Political economy">
         political economy
        </a>
        , applied to practical experience, covering the laws related to prices, wages, rent, exchange, revenue and taxes.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">
         Parliamentary
        </a>
        reports, with particular focus on commerce, agriculture and free trade.
       </li>
       <li>
        Reports and accounts of popular movements advocating free trade.
       </li>
       <li>
        General news from the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Court_of_St._James%27s" title="Court of St. James's">
         Court of St. James's
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Greater_London" title="Greater London">
         Metropolis
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Historic_counties_of_England" title="Historic counties of England">
         Provinces
        </a>
        , Scotland, and Ireland.
       </li>
       <li>
        Commercial topics such as changes in fiscal regulations, the state and prospects of the markets, imports and exports, foreign news, the state of the manufacturing districts, notices of important new mechanical improvements, shipping news, the money market, and the progress of railways and public companies.
       </li>
       <li>
        Agricultural topics, including the application of geology and
        <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">
         chemistry
        </a>
        ; notices of new and improved
        <a href="/wiki/List_of_agricultural_machinery" title="List of agricultural machinery">
         implements
        </a>
        , state of crops, markets, prices, foreign markets and prices converted into English money; from time to time, in some detail, the plans pursued in Belgium, Switzerland, and other well-cultivated countries.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_overseas_territories" title="British overseas territories">
         Colonial
        </a>
        and foreign topics, including trade, produce, political and fiscal changes, and other matters, including
        <a href="/wiki/Investigative_journalism" title="Investigative journalism">
         exposés
        </a>
        on the evils of restriction and protection, and the advantages of free intercourse and trade.
       </li>
       <li>
        Law reports, confined chiefly to areas important to commerce, manufacturing, and agriculture.
       </li>
       <li>
        Books, confined chiefly, but not so exclusively, to commerce, manufacturing, and agriculture, and including all treatises on political economy, finance, or taxation.
       </li>
       <li>
        A commercial
        <a href="/wiki/Gazette" title="Gazette">
         gazette
        </a>
        , with prices and statistics of the week.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Letter_to_the_editor" title="Letter to the editor">
         Correspondence and inquiries
        </a>
        from the news magazine's readers.
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       Wilson described it as taking part in "a severe contest between intelligence, which presses forward, and an unworthy, timid ignorance obstructing our progress", a phrase which still appears on its masthead as the publication's mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opinion_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-opinion-22">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It has long been respected as "one of the most competent and subtle Western periodicals on public affairs".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leites_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Leites-23">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The publication was a major source of financial and economic information for
       <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">
        Karl Marx
       </a>
       in the formulation of socialist theory; he wrote: "the London
       <i>
        Economist
       </i>
       , the European organ of the aristocracy of finance, described most strikingly the attitude of this class."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Its logo was designed in 1959 by
       <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_Stone" title="Reynolds Stone">
        Reynolds Stone
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_us_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-About_us-25">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In January 2012,
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       launched a new weekly section devoted exclusively to
       <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
        China
       </a>
       , the first new country section since the introduction of a section about the United States in 1942.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In August 2015, The Economist Group bought back 5 million of its shares (worth $284 million) from Pearson. Pearson's remaining shares (worth $447 million) would be sold to
       <a href="/wiki/Exor_(company)" title="Exor (company)">
        Exor
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_editors">
        List of editors
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: List of editors">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PSM_V12_D400_Walter_Bagehot.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1487" data-file-width="1187" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/PSM_V12_D400_Walter_Bagehot.jpg/220px-PSM_V12_D400_Walter_Bagehot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/PSM_V12_D400_Walter_Bagehot.jpg/330px-PSM_V12_D400_Walter_Bagehot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/PSM_V12_D400_Walter_Bagehot.jpg/440px-PSM_V12_D400_Walter_Bagehot.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PSM_V12_D400_Walter_Bagehot.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">
          Walter Bagehot
         </a>
         , one of the early Editors of
         <i>
          The Economist
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The editors of
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       have been:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/James_Wilson_(businessman)" title="James Wilson (businessman)">
         James Wilson
        </a>
        1843–1857 (
        <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">
         Herbert Spencer
        </a>
        was sub-editor from 1848 to 1853)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Richard_Holt_Hutton" title="Richard Holt Hutton">
         Richard Holt Hutton
        </a>
        1857–1861
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
         <a href="#cite_note-29">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">
         Walter Bagehot
        </a>
        , 1861–1877
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
         <a href="#cite_note-30">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Conner_Lathbury" title="Daniel Conner Lathbury">
         Daniel Conner Lathbury
        </a>
        , 1877–1881
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
         <a href="#cite_note-31">
          [30]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (
        <i>
         jointly
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Inglis_Palgrave" title="Inglis Palgrave">
         Inglis Palgrave
        </a>
        , 1877–1883 (
        <i>
         jointly
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Edward Johnstone, 1883–1907
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
         <a href="#cite_note-32">
          [31]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Francis_Wrigley_Hirst" title="Francis Wrigley Hirst">
         Francis Wrigley Hirst
        </a>
        , 1907–1916
       </li>
       <li>
        Hartley Withers, 1916–1921
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Walter_Layton,_1st_Baron_Layton" title="Walter Layton, 1st Baron Layton">
         Sir Walter Layton
        </a>
        , 1922–1938
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Crowther,_Baron_Crowther" title="Geoffrey Crowther, Baron Crowther">
         Geoffrey Crowther
        </a>
        , 1938–1956
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Donald_Tyerman" title="Donald Tyerman">
         Donald Tyerman
        </a>
        , 1956–1965
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Alastair_Burnet" title="Alastair Burnet">
         Sir Alastair Burnet
        </a>
        , 1965–1974
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Knight_(journalist)" title="Andrew Knight (journalist)">
         Andrew Knight
        </a>
        , 1974–1986
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Pennant-Rea" title="Rupert Pennant-Rea">
         Rupert Pennant-Rea
        </a>
        , 1986–1993
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Bill_Emmott" title="Bill Emmott">
         Bill Emmott
        </a>
        , 1993–2006
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/John_Micklethwait" title="John Micklethwait">
         John Micklethwait
        </a>
        , 2006–2014
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
         <a href="#cite_note-33">
          [32]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Zanny_Minton_Beddoes" title="Zanny Minton Beddoes">
         Zanny Minton Beddoes
        </a>
        , 2015–present
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
         <a href="#cite_note-34">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Opinions">
        Opinions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Opinions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/The_Economist_editorial_stance" title="The Economist editorial stance">
        The Economist editorial stance
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Economist_building_London4.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4230" data-file-width="3528" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Economist_building_London4.jpg/220px-Economist_building_London4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Economist_building_London4.jpg/330px-Economist_building_London4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Economist_building_London4.jpg/440px-Economist_building_London4.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Economist_building_London4.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Economist Building (until 2017), St James's Street, by
         <a href="/wiki/Alison_and_Peter_Smithson" title="Alison and Peter Smithson">
          Alison and Peter Smithson
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       When the news magazine was founded, the term "
       <a href="/wiki/Economism" title="Economism">
        economism
       </a>
       " denoted what would today be termed "
       <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">
        economic liberalism
       </a>
       ".
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       generally supports
       <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">
        free trade
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globalisation" title="Globalisation">
        globalisation
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_immigration" title="Free immigration">
        free immigration
       </a>
       . The activist and journalist
       <a href="/wiki/George_Monbiot" title="George Monbiot">
        George Monbiot
       </a>
       has described it as
       <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">
        neo-liberal
       </a>
       while occasionally accepting the propositions of
       <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">
        Keynesian economics
       </a>
       where deemed more "reasonable".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grauniad_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grauniad-36">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The news magazine favours a
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">
        carbon tax
       </a>
       to fight
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
        global warming
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to one former editor, Bill Emmott, "the
       <i>
        Economist
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       s philosophy has always been liberal, not conservative".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Individual contributors take diverse views.
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       favours the support, through
       <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">
        central banks
       </a>
       , of banks and other important corporations. This principle can, in a much more limited form, be traced back to
       <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">
        Walter Bagehot
       </a>
       , the third editor of
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       , who argued that the Bank of England should support major banks that got into difficulties.
       <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">
        Karl Marx
       </a>
       deemed
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       the "European organ" of "the aristocracy of finance".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The news magazine has also supported liberal causes on social issues such as recognition of gay marriages,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       legalisation of drugs,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       criticises the
       <a href="/wiki/Taxation_in_the_United_States" title="Taxation in the United States">
        US tax model
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and seems to support some government regulation on health issues, such as smoking in public,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as bans on spanking children.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       consistently favours guest worker programmes, parental choice of school, and amnesties
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and once published an "obituary" of God.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       also has a long record of supporting gun control.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       has endorsed the
       <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">
        Labour Party
       </a>
       (in 2005) the
       <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">
        Conservative Party
       </a>
       (in 2010 and 2015),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Economist_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-The_Economist-49">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">
        Liberal Democrats
       </a>
       (in 2017) at general election time in Britain, and both
       <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">
        Republican
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">
        Democratic
       </a>
       candidates in the United States.
       <i>
        Economist.com
       </i>
       puts its stance this way:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        What, besides free trade and free markets, does
        <i>
         The Economist
        </i>
        believe in? "It is to the Radicals that
        <i>
         The Economist
        </i>
        still likes to think of itself as belonging. The extreme centre is the paper's historical position". That is as true today as when Crowther [Geoffrey,
        <i>
         Economist
        </i>
        editor 1938–1956] said it in 1955.
        <i>
         The Economist
        </i>
        considers itself the enemy of privilege, pomposity and predictability. It has backed conservatives such as
        <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">
         Ronald Reagan
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">
         Margaret Thatcher
        </a>
        . It has supported the Americans in
        <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">
         Vietnam
        </a>
        . But it has also endorsed
        <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">
         Harold Wilson
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">
         Bill Clinton
        </a>
        , and espoused a variety of liberal causes: opposing capital punishment from its earliest days, while favouring penal reform and decolonisation, as well as—more recently—gun control and gay marriage.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_us_25-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-About_us-25">
          [24]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       frequently accuses figures and countries of corruption or dishonesty. In recent years, for example, it criticised
       <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wolfowitz" title="Paul Wolfowitz">
        Paul Wolfowitz
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">
        World Bank
       </a>
       president;
       <a href="/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi" title="Silvio Berlusconi">
        Silvio Berlusconi
       </a>
       , Italy's Prime Minister (who dubbed it
       <i>
        The Ecommunist
       </i>
       );
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Laurent-D%C3%A9sir%C3%A9_Kabila" title="Laurent-Désiré Kabila">
        Laurent-Désiré Kabila
       </a>
       , the late president of the
       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">
        Democratic Republic of the Congo
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">
        Robert Mugabe
       </a>
       , the head of government in
       <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">
        Zimbabwe
       </a>
       ; and, recently,
       <a href="/wiki/Cristina_Fern%C3%A1ndez_de_Kirchner" title="Cristina Fernández de Kirchner">
        Cristina Fernández de Kirchner
       </a>
       , the president of Argentina.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       also called for
       <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_of_Bill_Clinton" title="Impeachment of Bill Clinton">
        Bill Clinton's impeachment
       </a>
       and, after the emergence of the
       <a href="/wiki/Abu_Ghraib_torture_and_prisoner_abuse" title="Abu Ghraib torture and prisoner abuse">
        Abu Ghraib torture and prisoner abuse
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       for
       <a href="/wiki/Donald_Rumsfeld" title="Donald Rumsfeld">
        Donald Rumsfeld
       </a>
       's resignation. Though
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       initially gave vigorous support for the
       <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">
        US-led invasion of Iraq
       </a>
       , it later called the operation "bungled from the start" and criticised the "almost criminal negligence" of the Bush Administration's handling of the war, while maintaining, in 2007, that pulling out in the short term would be irresponsible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 2004 US election, the editors "reluctantly" backed John Kerry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 2008 US election
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       endorsed
       <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">
        Barack Obama
       </a>
       , while using the front cover of the issue published on the eve of the election to promote his candidacy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 2012 US election, Barack Obama was again endorsed: the editorial said that they preferred Obama on the economy, foreign policy and health care, but criticised him for running a negative campaign against Romney and for a "...poor appreciation of commerce."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tone_and_voice">
        Tone and voice
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Tone and voice">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Though it has many individual columns, by tradition and current practice the magazine ensures a uniform voice—aided by the anonymity of writers—throughout its pages,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as if most articles were written by a single author, which may be perceived to display dry, understated wit, and precise use of language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       has traditionally—albeit not always consistently—persisted in referring to itself as a "newspaper", rather than a newsmagazine, due to the gradual pace of its transformation in format from
       <a href="/wiki/Newsprint" title="Newsprint">
        newsprint
       </a>
       to glossy colour (articles were printed on the front page into the 1950s) in addition to its general focus on current affairs as opposed to specialist subjects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pgazette_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-pgazette-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       s treatment of economics presumes a working familiarity with fundamental concepts of classical economics. For instance, it does not explain terms like
       <a href="/wiki/Invisible_hand" title="Invisible hand">
        invisible hand
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Macroeconomics" title="Macroeconomics">
        macroeconomics
       </a>
       , or
       <a href="/wiki/Demand_curve" title="Demand curve">
        demand curve
       </a>
       , and may take just six or seven words to explain the theory of
       <a href="/wiki/Comparative_advantage" title="Comparative advantage">
        comparative advantage
       </a>
       . Articles involving economics do not presume any formal training on the part of the reader and aim to be accessible to the educated layman. It usually does not translate short French (and German) quotes or phrases. It does describe the business or nature of even well-known entities, writing, for example, "Goldman Sachs, an investment bank".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many articles include some witticism; image captions are often humorous puns and the letters section usually concludes with an odd or light-hearted letter. These efforts at humour have sometimes had a mixed reception. For example, the cover of the 20 September 2003 issue, headlined by a story on the
       <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">
        WTO
       </a>
       ministerial meeting in Cancún, featured a cactus
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finger_(gesture)" title="Finger (gesture)">
        giving the middle finger
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Readers sent both positive and negative letters in response.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Editorial_anonymity">
        Editorial anonymity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Editorial anonymity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Articles often take a definite editorial stance and almost never carry a
       <a href="/wiki/Byline" title="Byline">
        byline
       </a>
       . Not even the name of the editor (since 2015,
       <a href="/wiki/Zanny_Minton_Beddoes" title="Zanny Minton Beddoes">
        Zanny Minton Beddoes
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) is printed in the issue. It is a long-standing tradition that an editor's only signed article during their tenure is written on the occasion of their departure from the position. The author of a piece is named in certain circumstances: when notable persons are invited to contribute opinion pieces; when journalists of
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       compile special reports (previously known as surveys); for the Year in Review special edition; and to highlight a potential
       <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">
        conflict of interest
       </a>
       over a book review. The names of
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       editors and correspondents can be located on the media directory pages of the website.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Online blog pieces are signed with the initials of the writer and authors of print stories are allowed to note their authorship from their personal web sites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "This approach is not without its faults (we have four staff members with the initials "J.P.", for example) but is the best compromise between total anonymity and full bylines, in our view", wrote one anonymous writer of The Economist.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The editors say this is necessary because "collective voice and personality matter more than the identities of individual journalists"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and reflects "a collaborative effort".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In most articles, authors refer to themselves as "your correspondent" or "this reviewer". The writers of the titled opinion columns tend to refer to themselves by the title (hence, a sentence in the "Lexington" column might read "Lexington was informed...").
      </p>
      <p>
       The American author
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Lewis_(author)" title="Michael Lewis (author)">
        Michael Lewis
       </a>
       has criticised the magazine's editorial anonymity, labeling it a means to hide the youth and inexperience of those writing articles. In 1991 Lewis quipped: "The magazine is written by young people pretending to be old people ... If American readers got a look at the pimply complexions of their economic gurus, they would cancel their subscriptions in droves".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fallows_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-fallows-70">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Though widely believed, this description was (and is) factually false: the four editors appointed in 1965, 1974, 1986 and 1993 were all aged close to 37, no great age in journalism, even on appointment; and a fair number of their colleagues were older than they.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Although individual articles are written anonymously, there is no secrecy over who the writers are as they are listed on
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        's
       </span>
       website, which also provides summaries of their careers and academic qualifications.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/John_Ralston_Saul" title="John Ralston Saul">
        John Ralston Saul
       </a>
       describes
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       as a "...magazine which hides the names of the journalists who write its articles in order to create the illusion that they dispense disinterested truth rather than opinion. This sales technique, reminiscent of pre-Reformation Catholicism, is not surprising in a publication named after the social science most given to wild guesses and imaginary facts presented in the guise of inevitability and exactitude. That it is the Bible of the corporate executive indicates to what extent received wisdom is the daily bread of a managerial civilization."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Circulation">
        Circulation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Circulation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Each of
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       issue's official date range is from Saturday to the following Friday.
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       posts each week's new content online at approximately 2100 Thursday evening UK time, ahead of the official publication date.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1877, the publication's circulation was 3,700, and in 1920 it had risen to 6,000. Circulation increased rapidly after 1945, reaching 100,000 by 1970.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_us_25-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-About_us-25">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Circulation is audited by the
       <a href="/wiki/Audit_Bureau_of_Circulations_(UK)" title="Audit Bureau of Circulations (UK)">
        Audit Bureau of Circulations
       </a>
       (ABC). From around 30,000 in 1960 it has risen to near 1 million by 2000 and by 2016 to about 1.3 million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sales inside North America were in 2007 around 54 per cent of the total, with sales in the UK making up 14 per cent of the total and continental Europe 19 per cent.
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       claims sales, both by subscription and at newsagents, in over 200 countries. Of its American readers, two out of three earn more than $100,000 a year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gainsreaders_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gainsreaders-74">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       once boasted about its limited circulation. In the early 1990s it used the slogan "
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       – not read by millions of people". "Never in the history of journalism has so much been read for so long by so few," wrote
       <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Crowther,_Baron_Crowther" title="Geoffrey Crowther, Baron Crowther">
        Geoffrey Crowther
       </a>
       , a former editor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Economist Newspaper Limited is a wholly owned subsidiary of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Economist_Group" title="The Economist Group">
        The Economist Group
       </a>
       . The publications of the group include the
       <i>
        CFO
       </i>
       brand family as well as the annual
       <i>
        The World in...
       </i>
       , the lifestyle bimonthly
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/1843_(magazine)" title="1843 (magazine)">
         1843
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Voice" title="European Voice">
         European Voice
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Roll_Call" title="Roll Call">
         Roll Call
        </a>
       </i>
       . Sir
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evelyn_Robert_de_Rothschild" title="Evelyn Robert de Rothschild">
        Evelyn Robert de Rothschild
       </a>
       was Chairman of the company from 1972 to 1989.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Letters">
        Letters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Letters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       frequently receives letters from senior businesspeople, politicians, ambassadors, and from spokespeople for various government departments, non-governmental organisations and lobbies. Well-written or witty responses from anyone are considered, and controversial issues frequently produce a torrent of letters. For example, the survey of
       <a href="/wiki/Corporate_social_responsibility" title="Corporate social responsibility">
        corporate social responsibility
       </a>
       , published January 2005, produced largely critical letters from
       <a href="/wiki/Oxfam" title="Oxfam">
        Oxfam
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">
        World Food Programme
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Global_Compact" title="United Nations Global Compact">
        United Nations Global Compact
       </a>
       , the Chairman of
       <a href="/wiki/BT_Group" title="BT Group">
        BT Group
       </a>
       , an ex-Director of
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">
        Shell
       </a>
       and the UK
       <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Directors" title="Institute of Directors">
        Institute of Directors
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many of the letters published are critical of its stance or commentary. After
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       ran a critique of
       <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">
        Amnesty International
       </a>
       and human rights in general in its issue dated 24 March 2007, its letters page ran a vibrant reply from Amnesty, as well as several other letters in support of the organisation, including one from the head of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Commission_on_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Commission on Human Rights">
        United Nations Commission on Human Rights
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Rebuttals from officials within regimes such as the Singapore government are routinely printed, to comply with local
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right-of-reply" title="Right-of-reply">
        right-of-reply
       </a>
       laws without compromising editorial independence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It is extremely rare for any comment by
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       to appear alongside any published letter. Letters published in the news magazine are typically between 150 and 200 words long (and began with the salutation "Sir" until the editorship of Zanny Minton Beddoes, the first female editor; they now have no salutation). Previous to a change in procedure, all responses to on-line articles were usually published in "The Inbox".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Comments can now be made directly under each article.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Features">
        Features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Big_Mac_index_50USD_2columns.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1255" data-file-width="545" height="507" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Big_Mac_index_50USD_2columns.png/220px-Big_Mac_index_50USD_2columns.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Big_Mac_index_50USD_2columns.png/330px-Big_Mac_index_50USD_2columns.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Big_Mac_index_50USD_2columns.png/440px-Big_Mac_index_50USD_2columns.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Big_Mac_index_50USD_2columns.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Visualisation of the
         <a href="/wiki/Big_Mac_Index" title="Big Mac Index">
          Big Mac Index
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       s primary focus is world events, politics and business, but it also runs regular sections on science and technology as well as books and the arts. Approximately every two weeks, the publication includes an in-depth special report
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (previously called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Survey_article" title="Survey article">
        surveys
       </a>
       ) on a given topic. The five main categories are Countries and Regions, Business, Finance and Economics, Science and Technology, and Other. Every three months, it publishes a technology report called
       <i>
        Technology Quarterly
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or TQ, a special section focusing on recent trends and developments in science and technology.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since July 2007, there has also been a complete audio edition of the news magazine available 9pm London time on Thursdays.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The audio version of
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       is produced by the production company
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Talking_Issues&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Talking Issues (page does not exist)">
        Talking Issues
       </a>
       . The company records the full text of the news magazine in mp3 format, including the extra pages in the UK edition. The weekly 130 MB download is free for subscribers and available for a fee for non-subscribers.
      </p>
      <p>
       The publication's writers adopt a tight style that seeks to include the maximum amount of information in a limited space.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/David_G._Bradley" title="David G. Bradley">
        David G. Bradley
       </a>
       , publisher of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">
         The Atlantic
        </a>
       </i>
       , described the formula as "a consistent world view expressed, consistently, in tight and engaging prose".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is a section of
       <a href="/wiki/Economic_statistics" title="Economic statistics">
        economic statistics
       </a>
       . Tables such as employment statistics are published each week and there are special statistical features too. It is unique among British weeklies in providing authoritative coverage of official statistics and its rankings of international statistics have been decisive.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition,
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       is known for its
       <a href="/wiki/Big_Mac_Index" title="Big Mac Index">
        Big Mac Index
       </a>
       , which it first published in 1986, which uses the price of the hamburger in different countries as an informal measure of the
       <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">
        purchasing power
       </a>
       of currencies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The publication runs several opinion columns whose names reflect their topic:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         Analects
        </i>
        (China) – named after
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Analects" title="The Analects">
         The Analects
        </a>
        , a collection of
        <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">
         Confucian
        </a>
        sayings, this column was established in February 2012.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Bagehot
        </i>
        (Britain) – named for
        <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">
         Walter Bagehot
        </a>
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
          <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
           /
           <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
            <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
             ˈ
            </span>
            <span title="'b' in 'buy'">
             b
            </span>
            <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
             æ
            </span>
            <span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">
             dʒ
            </span>
            <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
             ə
            </span>
            <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
             t
            </span>
           </span>
           /
          </a>
         </span>
        </span>
        , 19th-century British constitutional expert and early editor of
        <i>
         The Economist
        </i>
        . From July 2010
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
         <a href="#cite_note-88">
          [87]
         </a>
        </sup>
        until June 2012
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
         <a href="#cite_note-89">
          [88]
         </a>
        </sup>
        it was written by
        <a href="/wiki/David_Rennie_(columnist)" title="David Rennie (columnist)">
         David Rennie
        </a>
        . Since April 2017 it has been written by
        <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Wooldridge" title="Adrian Wooldridge">
         Adrian Wooldridge
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Bello
        </i>
        (Latin America) – named for
        <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Bello" title="Andrés Bello">
         Andrés Bello
        </a>
        , a diplomat, poet, legislator and philosopher, who lived and worked in Chile.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
         <a href="#cite_note-90">
          [89]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The column was established in January 2014 and is written by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Reid_(journalist_and_writer)" title="Michael Reid (journalist and writer)">
         Michael Reid
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Charlemagne
        </i>
        (Europe) – named for
        <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
         Charlemagne
        </a>
        , Emperor of the
        <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">
         Frankish Empire
        </a>
        . It is written by Tom Nuttall and earlier it was written by David Rennie (2007–2010) and by Anton La Guardia
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
         <a href="#cite_note-91">
          [90]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (2010–2014).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Lexington
        </i>
        (United States) – named for
        <a href="/wiki/Lexington,_Massachusetts" title="Lexington, Massachusetts">
         Lexington, Massachusetts
        </a>
        , the site of the beginning of the
        <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">
         American Revolutionary War
        </a>
        . From June 2010 until May 2012 it was written by
        <a href="/wiki/Peter_David_(journalist)" title="Peter David (journalist)">
         Peter David
        </a>
        , until his death in a car accident.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
         <a href="#cite_note-92">
          [91]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Buttonwood
        </i>
        (Finance) – named for the
        <a href="/wiki/Platanus_occidentalis" title="Platanus occidentalis">
         buttonwood
        </a>
        tree where early
        <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">
         Wall Street
        </a>
        traders gathered. Until September 2006 this was available only as an on-line column, but it is now included in the print edition. It is written by
        <a href="/wiki/Philip_Coggan" title="Philip Coggan">
         Philip Coggan
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Banyan
        </i>
        (Asia) – named for the
        <a href="/wiki/Banyan" title="Banyan">
         banyan
        </a>
        tree, this column was established in April 2009 and focuses on various issues across the Asian continent, and is written by
        <a href="/wiki/Dominic_Ziegler" title="Dominic Ziegler">
         Dominic Ziegler
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Baobab
        </i>
        (Africa &amp; Middle East) – named for the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baobab" title="Baobab">
         baobab
        </a>
        tree, this column was established in July 2010 and focuses on various issues across the African continent.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Babbage
        </i>
        (Technology) – named for the inventor
        <a href="/wiki/Charles_Babbage" title="Charles Babbage">
         Charles Babbage
        </a>
        , this column was established in March 2010 and focuses on various technology related issues.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Prospero" title="Prospero">
          Prospero
         </a>
        </i>
        (Books and arts) – named after the character from
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare%27s" title="William Shakespeare's">
         William Shakespeare's
        </a>
        play
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Tempest" title="The Tempest">
          The Tempest
         </a>
        </i>
        , this column reviews books and focuses on arts-related issues.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Game Theory
        </i>
        (Sport) – named after
        <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">
         the science of predicting outcomes in a certain situation
        </a>
        , this column focuses on "sports major and minor" and "the politics, economics, science and statistics of the games we play and watch".
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Schumpeter
        </i>
        (Business) – named for the economist
        <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schumpeter" title="Joseph Schumpeter">
         Joseph Schumpeter
        </a>
        , this column was established in September 2009 and is written by Patrick Foulis.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Johnson
        </i>
        (language) – named for
        <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">
         Samuel Johnson
        </a>
        , this column returned to the publication in 2016 and covers language. It is written by
        <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lane_Greene" title="Robert Lane Greene">
         Robert Lane Greene
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Other regular features include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         Face Value
        </i>
        , about prominent people in the business world
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Free Exchange
        </i>
        , a general economics column, frequently based on academic research, replaced the column
        <i>
         Economics Focus
        </i>
        in January 2012
       </li>
       <li>
        An obituary. Since 1997 it has been written by Ann Wroe.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
         <a href="#cite_note-93">
          [92]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        sections on science and the arts
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The news magazine goes to press on Thursdays, between 6pm and 7pm GMT, and is available at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newsagent" title="Newsagent">
        newsagents
       </a>
       in many countries the next day. It is printed at seven sites around the world. Known on their website as "This week's print edition", it is available online, albeit with only the first five viewed articles being free (and available to subscribers only mid-October 2009 – 2010).
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       published in 2015 its first US college rankings, focused on comparable economical advantages defined as 'the economic value of a university is equal to the gap between how much its students subsequently earn, and how much they might have made had they studied elsewhere'. Based on set of strict criteria sourced from US Department of Education ("College Scorecard") with relevant 'expected earnings' and multiple statistics applied in calculation of 'median earnings' conclusive evaluation method has been applied to run the scorecard's earnings data through a multiple regression analysis, a common method of measuring the relationships between variables.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       also produces the annual
       <i>
        The World in
       </i>
       [
       <i>
        Year
       </i>
       ] publication. It also sponsors a writing award.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Economist_Awards">
        <i>
         The Economist
        </i>
        Awards
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The Economist Awards">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Innovation_Awards">
        Innovation Awards
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Innovation Awards">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:203px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Economist_innovation_awards_logo.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="144" data-file-width="201" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/9e/Economist_innovation_awards_logo.png" width="201"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Economist_innovation_awards_logo.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Innovation Awards Logo
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       sponsors the yearly "Economist Innovation Awards", in the categories of bioscience, computing and communications, energy and the environment, social and economic innovation, business-process innovation, consumer products, and a special "no boundaries" category.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The awards have been held since 2002. Nominations are held between 2 and 30 April. The award ceremony is then hosted on 15 November. Choices are based off the following factors:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        How much revenue their innovation has made their company or its economic impact on a specific good cause or society in general
       </li>
       <li>
        The effect their work has had on the marketplace (or if it's created a whole new marketplace altogether)
       </li>
       <li>
        The impact their innovation has had on a new type of science or technology
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_prize">
        Writing prize
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Writing prize">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1999,
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       organized a global futurist writing competition,
       <a href="/wiki/The_World_in_2050" title="The World in 2050">
        The World in 2050
       </a>
       . Co-sponsored by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch/Shell" title="Royal Dutch/Shell">
        Royal Dutch/Shell
       </a>
       , the competition included a first prize of
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">
        US$
       </a>
       20,000 and publication in
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       s annual flagship publication,
       <i>
        The World In
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newstraightstimes2000_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-newstraightstimes2000-97">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Over 3,000 entries from around the world were submitted via a website set up for the purpose and at various Royal Dutch Shell offices worldwide.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newstraightstimes2000_97-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-newstraightstimes2000-97">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The judging panel included
       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Emmott" title="Bill Emmott">
        Bill Emmott
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newstraightstimes2000_97-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-newstraightstimes2000-97">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Esther_Dyson" title="Esther Dyson">
        Esther Dyson
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sir
       <a href="/wiki/Mark_Moody-Stuart" title="Mark Moody-Stuart">
        Mark Moody-Stuart
       </a>
       (then-chairman of
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">
        Royal Dutch Shell
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newstraightstimes2000_97-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-newstraightstimes2000-97">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Matt_Ridley" title="Matt Ridley">
        Matt Ridley
       </a>
       (a British scientist and member of the
       <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">
        House of Lords
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Censorship">
        Censorship
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Censorship">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Sections of
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       criticising authoritarian regimes are frequently removed from the magazine by the authorities in those countries.
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       regularly has difficulties with the ruling party of
       <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">
        Singapore
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Action_Party" title="People's Action Party">
        People's Action Party
       </a>
       , which had successfully sued it, in a Singaporean court, for
       <a href="/wiki/Defamation" title="Defamation">
        libel
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       , like many other publications, is subjected to censorship in
       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
        India
       </a>
       whenever it depicts a map of
       <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">
        Kashmir
       </a>
       . The maps are stamped by Indian Customs officials as being "neither correct, nor authentic". Issues are sometimes delayed, but not stopped or seized.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 15 June 2006,
       <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
        Iran
       </a>
       banned the sale of
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       when it published a map labelling the
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">
        Persian Gulf
       </a>
       simply as "Gulf"—a choice that derives its political significance from the
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf_naming_dispute" title="Persian Gulf naming dispute">
        Persian Gulf naming dispute
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In a separate incident, the government of
       <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">
        Zimbabwe
       </a>
       went further and imprisoned
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       s correspondent there,
       <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Meldrum" title="Andrew Meldrum">
        Andrew Meldrum
       </a>
       . The government charged him with violating a statute on "publishing untruth" for writing that a woman was decapitated by supporters of the ruling
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_National_Union_%E2%80%93_Patriotic_Front" title="Zimbabwe African National Union – Patriotic Front">
        Zimbabwe African National Union – Patriotic Front
       </a>
       party. The
       <a href="/wiki/Decapitation" title="Decapitation">
        decapitation
       </a>
       claim was retracted
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and allegedly fabricated by the woman's husband. The correspondent was later acquitted, only to receive a
       <a href="/wiki/Deportation" title="Deportation">
        deportation
       </a>
       order.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 19 August 2013,
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       disclosed that the Missouri Department of Corrections had censored its issue of 29 June 2013. According to the letter sent by the department, prisoners were not allowed to receive the issue because "1. it constitutes a threat to the security or discipline of the institution; 2. may facilitate or encourage criminal activity; or 3. may interfere with the rehabilitation of an offender".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Criticism.2C_accusation_and_praise">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism,_accusation_and_praise">
        Criticism, accusation and praise
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Criticism, accusation and praise">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In 1991,
       <a href="/wiki/James_Fallows" title="James Fallows">
        James Fallows
       </a>
       argued in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">
         The Washington Post
        </a>
       </i>
       that
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       used editorial lines that contradicted the news stories they purported to highlight.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fallows_70-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-fallows-70">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1999,
       <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Sullivan" title="Andrew Sullivan">
        Andrew Sullivan
       </a>
       complained in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">
         The New Republic
        </a>
       </i>
       that it uses "marketing genius"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to make up for deficiencies in analysis and original reporting, resulting in "a kind of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Reader%27s_Digest" title="Reader's Digest">
         Reader's Digest
        </a>
       </i>
       "
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_106-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-106">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       for America's corporate elite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although he acknowledged that the magazine's claim about the dotcom bubble bursting would probably be accurate in the long run (the bubble burst in the US market two years later),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sullivan pointed out that the magazine greatly exaggerated the danger the US economy was in after the Dow Jones fell to 7,400 during the 1998 Labor Day weekend. He also said that
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       is editorially constrained because so many scribes graduated from the same college at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University">
        Oxford University
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Magdalen_College,_Oxford" title="Magdalen College, Oxford">
        Magdalen College
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_106-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-106">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">
         The Guardian
        </a>
       </i>
       wrote that "its writers rarely see a political or economic problem that cannot be solved by the trusted three-card trick of privatisation, deregulation and liberalisation".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_109-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-109">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2008,
       <a href="/wiki/Jon_Meacham" title="Jon Meacham">
        Jon Meacham
       </a>
       , former editor of
       <i>
        Newsweek
       </i>
       and a self-described "fan", criticised
       <i>
        The Economist'
       </i>
       s focus on analysis over original reporting.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
        <a href="#cite_note-110">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2012,
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       was
       <a href="/wiki/2012_ICT_Skype_controversy" title="2012 ICT Skype controversy">
        accused
       </a>
       of hacking into the computer of Justice
       <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Nizamul_Huq" title="Mohammed Nizamul Huq">
        Mohammed Nizamul Huq
       </a>
       of the Bangladesh Supreme Court, leading to his resignation as the chairman of the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Crimes_Tribunal_(Bangladesh)" title="International Crimes Tribunal (Bangladesh)">
        International Crimes Tribunal
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICTleaks_111-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ICTleaks-111">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICTleaks1_112-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ICTleaks1-112">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICTleaks2_113-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ICTleaks2-113">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICTleaks3_114-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ICTleaks3-114">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICTleaks4_115-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ICTleaks4-115">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICTleaks5_116-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ICTleaks5-116">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The magazine denied the accusations.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2014, the magazine withdrew a harshly-criticised review of a book by
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_E._Baptist" title="Edward E. Baptist">
        Edward Baptist
       </a>
       on slavery and American capitalism;
       <i>
        The Economist
       </i>
       had complained that "[a]lmost all the blacks in his book are victims, almost all the whites villains."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Baptist attributed the harsh review to the magazine's adherence to "
       <a href="/wiki/Market_fundamentalism" title="Market fundamentalism">
        free-market fundamentalist
       </a>
       " theories, "the idea that everything would be better if measured first and last by its efficiency at producing profit."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Praise_and_accolades">
        Praise and accolades
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Praise and accolades">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 2005, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">
         Chicago Tribune
        </a>
       </i>
       named it the best English-language magazine noting its strength in international reporting "where it does not feel moved to cover a faraway land only at a time of unmitigated disaster" and that it kept a wall between its reporting and its more conservative editorial policies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
        <a href="#cite_note-119">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-1">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-1">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The title and its design are references to the book
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/No_Logo" title="No Logo">
             No Logo
            </a>
           </i>
           (1999).
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>

        </b>
       </div>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="official-website">
         <span class="url">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.economist.com/" rel="nofollow">
           Official website
          </a>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.economistgroup.com/" rel="nofollow">
         The Economist Group
        </a>
        website providing group information and links to all group publications such as
        <i>
         CFO
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         Roll Call
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         European Voice
        </i>
       </li>

        <i>
         The Economist
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.economist.com/displaystory.cfm?story_id=1873490" rel="nofollow">
         Preliminary number and prospectus, 5 August 1843
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=oN0DAAAAQAAJ" rel="nofollow">
         Capital, currency, and banking; a series of articles published in
         <i>
          The Economist
         </i>

        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Wilson_(UK_politician)" title="James Wilson (UK politician)">

        </a>

       <li>
